</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:General_geometry" title="Template:General geometry"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:General_geometry" title="Template talk:General geometry"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:General_geometry&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Three-dimensional space</b> (also: <b>3D space</b>, <b>3-space</b> or, rarely, <b>tri-dimensional space</b>) is a geometric setting in which three values (called <i><a href="/wiki/Parameter" title="Parameter">parameters</a></i>) are required to determine the <a href="/wiki/Position_(geometry)" title="Position (geometry)">position</a> of an element (i.e., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Point_(mathematics)" title="Point (mathematics)">point</a>). This is the informal meaning of the term <a href="/wiki/Dimension" title="Dimension">dimension</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tuple" title="Tuple">tuple</a> of <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">numbers</a> can be understood as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinates" title="Cartesian coordinates">Cartesian coordinates</a> of a location in a <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span>-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_space" title="Euclidean space">Euclidean space</a>. The set of these <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">n</span>-tuples is commonly denoted <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{n},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{n},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{n},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d7035fcb9fe3ebecc6bc9f372f82d0352202c8bf" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.543ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> and can be identified to the <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">n</span>-dimensional Euclidean space.
When <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i> = 3</span>, this space is called <span class="anchor" id="3D_EUCLIDEAN_SPACE"></span><b>three-dimensional Euclidean space</b> (or simply Euclidean space when the context is clear).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> It serves as a model of the physical <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> (when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativity_theory" title="Relativity theory">relativity theory</a> is not considered), in which all known <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> exists. While this space remains the most compelling and useful way to model the world as it is experienced,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> it is only one example of a large variety of spaces in three dimensions called <a href="/wiki/3-manifold" title="3-manifold">3-manifolds</a>. In this classical example, when the three values refer to measurements in different directions (<a href="/wiki/Coordinate_system" title="Coordinate system">coordinates</a>), any three directions can be chosen, provided that <a href="/wiki/Vector_space" title="Vector space">vectors</a> in these directions do not all lie in the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two-dimensional_space" title="Two-dimensional space">2-space</a> (<a href="/wiki/Plane_(geometry)" title="Plane (geometry)">plane</a>). Furthermore, in this case, these three values can be labeled by any combination of three chosen from the terms <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Width" title="Width">width</a>/breadth</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Height" title="Height">height</a>/depth</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Length" title="Length">length</a></i>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Books XI to XIII of <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Euclid's Elements</a> dealt with three-dimensional geometry. Book XI develops notions of orthogonality and parallelism of lines and planes, and defines solids including parallelpipeds, pyramids, prisms, spheres, octahedra, icosahedra and dodecahedra. Book XII develops notions of similarity of solids. Book XIII describes the construction of the five regular <a href="/wiki/Platonic_solid" title="Platonic solid">Platonic solids</a> in a sphere.
</p><p>In the 17th century, three-dimensional space was described with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinates" title="Cartesian coordinates">Cartesian coordinates</a>, with the advent of <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytic geometry</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> in his work <i><a href="/wiki/La_G%C3%A9om%C3%A9trie" title="La Géométrie">La Géométrie</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat" title="Pierre de Fermat">Pierre de Fermat</a> in the manuscript <i>Ad locos planos et solidos isagoge</i> (Introduction to Plane and Solid Loci), which was unpublished during Fermat's lifetime. However, only Fermat's work dealt with three-dimensional space.
</p><p>In the 19th century, developments of the geometry of three-dimensional space came with <a href="/wiki/William_Rowan_Hamilton" title="William Rowan Hamilton">William Rowan Hamilton</a>'s development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaternions" title="Quaternions">quaternions</a>. In fact, it was Hamilton who coined the terms <a href="/wiki/Scalar_(mathematics)" title="Scalar (mathematics)">scalar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_vector" title="Euclidean vector">vector</a>, and they were first defined within <a href="/wiki/Classical_Hamiltonian_quaternions" title="Classical Hamiltonian quaternions">his geometric framework for quaternions</a>. Three dimensional space could then be described by quaternions <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle q=a+ui+vj+wk}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>q</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>u</mi>
<mi>i</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>v</mi>
<mi>j</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>w</mi>
<mi>k</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle q=a+ui+vj+wk}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle q=a+ui+vj+wk}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/066b54eb5f919cf41dc32554f6d74ba2daea8bc0" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:21.012ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> which had vanishing scalar component, that is, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>a</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a=0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="a=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/90d476e5e765a5d77bbcff32e4584579207ec7d8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.491ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. While not explicitly studied by Hamilton, this indirectly introduced notions of basis, here given by the quaternion elements <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle i,j,k}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>i</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>j</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>k</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle i,j,k}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="i,j,k" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9550e66fe7e601c4f58bbc9c19ba226301149cde" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:5.04ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Dot_product" title="Dot product">dot product</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cross_product" title="Cross product">cross product</a>, which correspond to (the negative of) the scalar part and the vector part of the product of two vector quaternions.
</p><p>It wasn't until <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Willard_Gibbs" title="Josiah Willard Gibbs">Josiah Willard Gibbs</a> that these two products were identified in their own right, and the modern notation for the dot and cross product were introduced in his classroom teaching notes, found also in the 1901 textbook <i><a href="/wiki/Vector_Analysis" title="Vector Analysis">Vector Analysis</a></i> written by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Bidwell_Wilson" title="Edwin Bidwell Wilson">Edwin Bidwell Wilson</a> based on Gibbs' lectures.
</p><p>Also during the 19th century came developments in the abstract formalism of vector spaces, with the work of <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Grassmann" title="Hermann Grassmann">Hermann Grassmann</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Peano" title="Giuseppe Peano">Giuseppe Peano</a>, the latter of whom first gave the modern definition of vector spaces as an algebraic structure.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Euclidean_geometry">In Euclidean geometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: In Euclidean geometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordinate_systems">Coordinate systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Coordinate systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_system" title="Coordinate system">Coordinate system</a></div>
<p>In mathematics, <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytic geometry</a> (also called Cartesian geometry) describes every point in three-dimensional space by means of three coordinates. Three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coordinate_axis" title="Coordinate axis">coordinate axes</a> are given, each perpendicular to the other two at the <a href="/wiki/Origin_(mathematics)" title="Origin (mathematics)">origin</a>, the point at which they cross. They are usually labeled <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i>, <i>y</i></span>, and <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i></span>. Relative to these axes, the position of any point in three-dimensional space is given by an ordered triple of <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a>, each number giving the distance of that point from the <a href="/wiki/Origin_(mathematics)" title="Origin (mathematics)">origin</a> measured along the given axis, which is equal to the distance of that point from the plane determined by the other two axes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other popular methods of describing the location of a point in three-dimensional space include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cylindrical_coordinates" title="Cylindrical coordinates">cylindrical coordinates</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spherical_coordinates" title="Spherical coordinates">spherical coordinates</a>, though there are an infinite number of possible methods. For more, see <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_space" title="Euclidean space">Euclidean space</a>.
</p><p>Below are images of the above-mentioned systems.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lines_and_planes">Lines and planes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lines and planes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Two distinct points always determine a (straight) <a href="/wiki/Line_(geometry)" title="Line (geometry)">line</a>. Three distinct points are either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collinear" title="Collinear">collinear</a> or determine a unique plane. On the other hand, four distinct points can either be collinear, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coplanar" title="Coplanar">coplanar</a>, or determine the entire space.
</p><p>Two distinct lines can either intersect, be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parallel_line" title="Parallel line">parallel</a> or be <a href="/wiki/Skew_lines" title="Skew lines">skew</a>. Two parallel lines, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two_intersecting_lines" title="Two intersecting lines">two intersecting lines</a>, lie in a unique plane, so skew lines are lines that do not meet and do not lie in a common plane.
</p><p>Two distinct planes can either meet in a common line or are parallel (i.e., do not meet). Three distinct planes, no pair of which are parallel, can either meet in a common line, meet in a unique common point, or have no point in common. In the last case, the three lines of intersection of each pair of planes are mutually parallel.
</p><p>A line can lie in a given plane, intersect that plane in a unique point, or be parallel to the plane. In the last case, there will be lines in the plane that are parallel to the given line.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Hyperplane" title="Hyperplane">hyperplane</a> is a subspace of one dimension less than the dimension of the full space. The hyperplanes of a three-dimensional space are the two-dimensional subspaces, that is, the planes. In terms of Cartesian coordinates, the points of a hyperplane satisfy a single <a href="/wiki/Linear_equation" title="Linear equation">linear equation</a>, so planes in this 3-space are described by linear equations. A line can be described by a pair of independent linear equations—each representing a plane having this line as a common intersection.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Varignon%27s_theorem" title="Varignon's theorem">Varignon's theorem</a> states that the midpoints of any quadrilateral in ℝ<sup>3</sup> form a <a href="/wiki/Parallelogram" title="Parallelogram">parallelogram</a>, and hence are coplanar.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spheres_and_balls">Spheres and balls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Spheres and balls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">Sphere</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sphere_wireframe_10deg_6r.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Sphere_wireframe_10deg_6r.svg/220px-Sphere_wireframe_10deg_6r.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Sphere_wireframe_10deg_6r.svg/330px-Sphere_wireframe_10deg_6r.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Sphere_wireframe_10deg_6r.svg/440px-Sphere_wireframe_10deg_6r.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sphere_wireframe_10deg_6r.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/3D_projection#Perspective_projection" title="3D projection">perspective projection</a> of a sphere onto two dimensions</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a> in 3-space (also called a <b>2-sphere</b> because it is a 2-dimensional object) consists of the set of all points in 3-space at a fixed distance <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span> from a central point <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">P</span>. The solid enclosed by the sphere is called a <a href="/wiki/Ball_(mathematics)" title="Ball (mathematics)"><b>ball</b></a> (or, more precisely a <b>3-ball</b>).
</p><p>The volume of the ball is given by
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle V={\frac {4}{3}}\pi r^{3},}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>V</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>4</mn>
<mn>3</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<msup>
<mi>r</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle V={\frac {4}{3}}\pi r^{3},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle V={\frac {4}{3}}\pi r^{3},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fa8faee726841ab4b331348f4ae7294c71f84207" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:10.966ex; height:5.176ex;"/></div>
and the surface area of the sphere is
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle A=4\pi r^{2}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</msup>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle A=4\pi r^{2}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle A=4\pi r^{2}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/95625828c519b36791d56af65d21b8448472650d" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:10.086ex; height:2.676ex;"/></div>
Another type of sphere arises from a 4-ball, whose three-dimensional surface is the <b>3-sphere</b>: points equidistant to the origin of the euclidean space <span class="texhtml">ℝ<sup>4</sup></span>. If a point has coordinates, <span class="texhtml"><i>P</i>(<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>, <i>z</i>, <i>w</i>)</span>, then <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i><sup>2</sup> + <i>y</i><sup>2</sup> + <i>z</i><sup>2</sup> + <i>w</i><sup>2</sup> = 1</span> characterizes those points on the unit 3-sphere centered at the origin.
</p><p>This 3-sphere is an example of a 3-manifold: a space which is 'looks locally' like 3D space. In precise topological terms, each point of the 3-sphere has a neighborhood which is homeomorphic to an open subset of 3D space.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polytopes">Polytopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Polytopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polyhedron" title="Polyhedron">Polyhedron</a></div>
<p>In three dimensions, there are nine regular polytopes: the five convex <a href="/wiki/Platonic_solid" title="Platonic solid">Platonic solids</a> and the four nonconvex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kepler-Poinsot_polyhedra" title="Kepler-Poinsot polyhedra">Kepler-Poinsot polyhedra</a>.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surfaces_of_revolution">Surfaces of revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Surfaces of revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Surface_of_revolution" title="Surface of revolution">Surface of revolution</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Surface_(topology)" title="Surface (topology)">surface</a> generated by revolving a plane <a href="/wiki/Curve" title="Curve">curve</a> about a fixed line in its plane as an axis is called a <a href="/wiki/Surface_of_revolution" title="Surface of revolution">surface of revolution</a>. The plane curve is called the <i><a href="/wiki/Generatrix" title="Generatrix">generatrix</a></i> of the surface. A section of the surface, made by intersecting the surface with a plane that is perpendicular (orthogonal) to the axis, is a circle.
</p><p>Simple examples occur when the generatrix is a line. If the generatrix line intersects the axis line, the surface of revolution is a right circular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cone_(mathematics)" title="Cone (mathematics)">cone</a> with vertex (apex) the point of intersection. However, if the generatrix and axis are parallel, then the surface of revolution is a circular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cylinder_(geometry)" title="Cylinder (geometry)">cylinder</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quadric_surfaces">Quadric surfaces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Quadric surfaces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadric_surface" title="Quadric surface">Quadric surface</a></div>
<p>In analogy with the <a href="/wiki/Conic_section" title="Conic section">conic sections</a>, the set of points whose Cartesian coordinates satisfy the general equation of the second degree, namely,
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle Ax^{2}+By^{2}+Cz^{2}+Fxy+Gyz+Hxz+Jx+Ky+Lz+M=0,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>A</mi>
<msup>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>B</mi>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mo>+</mo>

<mn>0</mn>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Ax^{2}+By^{2}+Cz^{2}+Fxy+Gyz+Hxz+Jx+Ky+Lz+M=0,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle Ax^{2}+By^{2}+Cz^{2}+Fxy+Gyz+Hxz+Jx+Ky+Lz+M=0,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e94ff5a2d23a9e2b80bcd3c7b471b57f33c0a144" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:66.401ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i>, <i>B</i>, <i>C</i>, <i>F</i>, <i>G</i>, <i>H</i>, <i>J</i>, <i>K</i>, <i>L</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>M</i></span> are real numbers and not all of <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i>, <i>B</i>, <i>C</i>, <i>F</i>, <i>G</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>H</i></span> are zero, is called a <b>quadric surface</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brannan_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brannan-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are six types of <a href="/wiki/Degeneracy_(mathematics)" title="Degeneracy (mathematics)">non-degenerate</a> quadric surfaces:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">Ellipsoid</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperboloid_of_one_sheet" title="Hyperboloid of one sheet">Hyperboloid of one sheet</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperboloid_of_two_sheets" title="Hyperboloid of two sheets">Hyperboloid of two sheets</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliptic_cone" title="Elliptic cone">Elliptic cone</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliptic_paraboloid" title="Elliptic paraboloid">Elliptic paraboloid</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_paraboloid" title="Hyperbolic paraboloid">Hyperbolic paraboloid</a></li></ol>
<p>The degenerate quadric surfaces are the empty set, a single point, a single line, a single plane, a pair of planes or a quadratic cylinder (a surface consisting of a non-degenerate conic section in a plane <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span> and all the lines of <span class="texhtml">ℝ<sup>3</sup></span> through that conic that are normal to <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brannan_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brannan-4">[4]</a></sup> Elliptic cones are sometimes considered to be degenerate quadric surfaces as well.
</p><p>Both the hyperboloid of one sheet and the hyperbolic paraboloid are <a href="/wiki/Ruled_surface" title="Ruled surface">ruled surfaces</a>, meaning that they can be made up from a family of straight lines. In fact, each has two families of generating lines, the members of each family are disjoint and each member one family intersects, with just one exception, every member of the other family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Each family is called a <a href="/wiki/Regulus_(geometry)" title="Regulus (geometry)">regulus</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_linear_algebra">In linear algebra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: In linear algebra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Another way of viewing three-dimensional space is found in <a href="/wiki/Linear_algebra" title="Linear algebra">linear algebra</a>, where the idea of independence is crucial. Space has three dimensions because the length of a <a href="/wiki/Cuboid" title="Cuboid">box</a> is independent of its width or breadth. In the technical language of linear algebra, space is three-dimensional because every point in space can be described by a linear combination of three independent <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_vector" title="Coordinate vector">vectors</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Dot_product.2C_angle.2C_and_length"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dot_product,_angle,_and_length">Dot product, angle, and length</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Dot product, angle, and length">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dot_product" title="Dot product">Dot product</a></div>
<p>A vector can be pictured as an arrow. The vector's magnitude is its length, and its direction is the direction the arrow points. A vector in <span class="texhtml">ℝ<sup>3</sup></span> can be represented by an ordered triple of real numbers. These numbers are called the <b>components</b> of the vector.
</p><p>The dot product of two vectors <span class="texhtml"><b>A</b> = [<i>A</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>A</i><sub>2</sub>, <i>A</i><sub><i>3</i></sub>]</span> and <span class="texhtml"><b>B</b> = [<i>B</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>B</i><sub>2</sub>, <i>B</i><sub><i>3</i></sub>]</span> is defined as:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbf {A} \cdot \mathbf {B} =\|\mathbf {A} \|\,\|\mathbf {B} \|\cos \theta ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6bb1da1f07c00da888269e2fb483494fabc2efb5" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:23.278ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <span class="texhtml"><i>θ</i></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Angle" title="Angle">angle</a> between <span class="texhtml"><b>A</b></span> and <span class="texhtml"><b>B</b></span>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cross_product">Cross product</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cross product">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cross_product" title="Cross product">Cross product</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cross_product" title="Cross product">cross product</a> or <b>vector product</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Binary_operation" title="Binary operation">binary operation</a> on two <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_vector" title="Euclidean vector">vectors</a> in three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_space" title="Euclidean space">space</a> and is denoted by the symbol  ×. The cross product <b>A</b> × <b>B</b> of the vectors <b>A</b> and <b>B</b> is a vector that is <a href="/wiki/Perpendicular" title="Perpendicular">perpendicular</a> to both and therefore <a href="/wiki/Normal_(geometry)" title="Normal (geometry)">normal</a> to the plane containing them. It has many applications in mathematics, <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>.
</p><p>In function language, the cross product is a function <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \times :\mathbb {R} ^{3}\times \mathbb {R} ^{3}\rightarrow \mathbb {R} ^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

It has the property that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {A} \times \mathbf {B} =-\mathbf {B} \times \mathbf {A} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>Its magnitude is related to the angle <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \theta }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>The space and product form an <a href="/wiki/Algebra_over_a_field" title="Algebra over a field">algebra over a field</a>, which is not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commutative" title="Commutative">commutative</a> nor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Associative" title="Associative">associative</a>, but is a <a href="/wiki/Lie_algebra" title="Lie algebra">Lie algebra</a> with the cross product being the Lie bracket. Specifically, the space together with the product, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle (\mathbb {R} ^{3},\times )}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>,</mo>
<mo>×<!-- × --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (\mathbb {R} ^{3},\times )}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle (\mathbb {R} ^{3},\times )}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1ab18c78ffa1af46269dfa7c50e96e7cc84c82dd" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.384ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isomorphic" title="Isomorphic">isomorphic</a> to the Lie algebra of three-dimensional rotations, denoted <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\mathfrak {so}}(3)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

In order to satisfy the axioms of a Lie algebra, instead of associativity the cross product satisfies the <a href="/wiki/Jacobi_identity" title="Jacobi identity">Jacobi identity</a>. For any three vectors <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {A} ,\mathbf {B} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<semantics>

</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {A} \times (\mathbf {B} \times \mathbf {C} )+\mathbf {B} \times (\mathbf {C} \times \mathbf {A} )+\mathbf {C} \times (\mathbf {A} \times \mathbf {B} )=0}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbf {A} \times (\mathbf {B} \times \mathbf {C} )+\mathbf {B} \times (\mathbf {C} \times \mathbf {A} )+\mathbf {C} \times (\mathbf {A} \times \mathbf {B} )=0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbf {A} \times (\mathbf {B} \times \mathbf {C} )+\mathbf {B} \times (\mathbf {C} \times \mathbf {A} )+\mathbf {C} \times (\mathbf {A} \times \mathbf {B} )=0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1941d61d2536babf6d243e3c277ef63d542af047" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:49.967ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div>
</p><p>One can in <i>n</i> dimensions take the product of <span class="nowrap"><i>n</i> − 1</span> vectors to produce a vector perpendicular to all of them. But if the product is limited to non-trivial binary products with vector results, it exists only in three and <a href="/wiki/Seven-dimensional_cross_product" title="Seven-dimensional cross product">seven dimensions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Massey2_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Massey2-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abstract_description">Abstract description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Abstract description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Vector_space" title="Vector space">vector space</a></div>
<p>It can be useful to describe three-dimensional space as a three-dimensional vector space <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle V}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>

This differs from <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

By definition, there exists a basis <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\mathcal {B}}=\{e_{1},e_{2},e_{3}\}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

This corresponds to an <a href="/wiki/Isomorphism" title="Isomorphism">isomorphism</a> between <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle V}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mo>=</mo>

<msub>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
</msub>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E_{1}={\begin{pmatrix}1\\0\\0\end{pmatrix}},E_{2}={\begin{pmatrix}0\\1\\0\end{pmatrix}},E_{3}={\begin{pmatrix}0\\0\\1\end{pmatrix}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle E_{1}={\begin{pmatrix}1\\0\\0\end{pmatrix}},E_{2}={\begin{pmatrix}0\\1\\0\end{pmatrix}},E_{3}={\begin{pmatrix}0\\0\\1\end{pmatrix}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/00c3f0ec012e6036c1ced792e94b6af5db9f0000" style="vertical-align: -4.005ex; width:38.261ex; height:9.176ex;"/></div>
</p><p>Therefore <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} ^{3}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f936ddf584f8f3dd2a0ed08917001b7a404c10b5" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.732ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> can be viewed as the abstract vector space, together with the additional structure of a choice of basis. Conversely, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle V}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>V</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle V}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="V" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/af0f6064540e84211d0ffe4dac72098adfa52845" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.787ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> can be obtained by starting with <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} ^{3}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f936ddf584f8f3dd2a0ed08917001b7a404c10b5" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.732ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> and 'forgetting' the Cartesian product structure, or equivalently the standard choice of basis.
</p><p>As opposed to a general vector space <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle V}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>V</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle V}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="V" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/af0f6064540e84211d0ffe4dac72098adfa52845" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.787ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, the space <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} ^{3}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f936ddf584f8f3dd2a0ed08917001b7a404c10b5" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.732ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> is sometimes referred to as a coordinate space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Physically, it is conceptually desirable to use the abstract formalism in order to assume as little structure as possible if it is not given by the parameters of a particular problem. For example, in a problem with rotational symmetry, working with the more concrete description of three-dimensional space <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} ^{3}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f936ddf584f8f3dd2a0ed08917001b7a404c10b5" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.732ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> assumes a choice of basis, corresponding to a set of axes. But in rotational symmetry, there is no reason why one set of axes is preferred to say, the same set of axes which has been rotated arbitrarily. Stated another way, a preferred choice of axes breaks the rotational symmetry of physical space.
</p><p>Computationally, it is necessary to work with the more concrete description <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} ^{3}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f936ddf584f8f3dd2a0ed08917001b7a404c10b5" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.732ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> in order to do concrete computations.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Affine_description">Affine description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Affine description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Affine_space" title="Affine space">affine space</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_space" title="Euclidean space">Euclidean space</a></div>
<p>A more abstract description still is to model physical space as a three-dimensional affine space <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle E(3)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>E</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mn>3</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E(3)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle E(3)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e58743110efa7ef2ff0907b98d20b1b5f5f26edf" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.747ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> over the real numbers. This is unique up to affine isomorphism. It is sometimes referred to as three-dimensional Euclidean space. Just as the vector space description came from 'forgetting the preferred basis' of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} ^{3}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f936ddf584f8f3dd2a0ed08917001b7a404c10b5" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.732ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>, the affine space description comes from 'forgetting the origin' of the vector space. Euclidean spaces are sometimes called <i>Euclidean affine spaces</i> for distinguishing them from Euclidean vector spaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerger1987Chapter_9_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerger1987Chapter_9-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>This is physically appealing as it makes the translation invariance of physical space manifest. A preferred origin breaks the translational invariance.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_product_space">Inner product space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Inner product space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Inner_product_space" title="Inner product space">inner product space</a></div>
<p>The above discussion does not involve the <a href="/wiki/Dot_product" title="Dot product">dot product</a>. The dot product is an example of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_product" title="Inner product">inner product</a>. Physical space can be modelled as a vector space which additionally has the structure of an inner product. The inner product defines notions of length and angle (and therefore in particular the notion of orthogonality). For any inner product, there exist bases under which the inner product agrees with the dot product, but again, there are many different possible bases, none of which are preferred. They differ from one another by a rotation, an element of the group of rotations <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SO(3)" title="SO(3)">SO(3)</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_calculus">In calculus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: In calculus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vector_calculus" title="Vector calculus">vector calculus</a></div>
<h3><span id="Gradient.2C_divergence_and_curl"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gradient,_divergence_and_curl">Gradient, divergence and curl</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Gradient, divergence and curl">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h3><span id="Line_integrals.2C_surface_integrals.2C_and_volume_integrals"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Line_integrals,_surface_integrals,_and_volume_integrals">Line integrals, surface integrals, and volume integrals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Line integrals, surface integrals, and volume integrals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For some <a href="/wiki/Scalar_field" title="Scalar field">scalar field</a> <i>f</i> : <i>U</i> ⊆ <b>R</b><sup><i>n</i></sup> → <b>R</b>, the line integral along a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piecewise_smooth" title="Piecewise smooth">piecewise smooth</a> <a href="/wiki/Curve" title="Curve">curve</a> <i>C</i> ⊂  <i>U</i> is defined as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \int \limits _{C}f\,ds=\int _{a}^{b}f(\mathbf {r} (t))|\mathbf {r} '(t)|\,dt.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\int\limits_C f\, ds = \int_a^b f(\mathbf{r}(t)) |\mathbf{r}'(t)|\, dt." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0be5379e6e88e64eaba171839d88f4446697b34c" style="vertical-align: -4.005ex; margin-left: -0.031ex; width:29.128ex; height:8.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <b>r</b>: [a, b] → <i>C</i> is an arbitrary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bijective" title="Bijective">bijective</a> <a href="/wiki/Parametric_equation" title="Parametric equation">parametrization</a> of the curve <i>C</i> such that <b>r</b>(<i>a</i>) and <b>r</b>(<i>b</i>) give the endpoints of <i>C</i> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a&lt;b}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="a&lt;b" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/91a7698e4c7401bb321f97888b872b583a9e4642" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.326ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>.
</p><p>For a <a href="/wiki/Vector_field" title="Vector field">vector field</a> <b>F</b> : <i>U</i> ⊆ <b>R</b><sup><i>n</i></sup> → <b>R</b><sup><i>n</i></sup>, the line integral along a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piecewise_smooth" title="Piecewise smooth">piecewise smooth</a> <a href="/wiki/Curve" title="Curve">curve</a> <i>C</i> ⊂ <i>U</i>, in the direction of <b>r</b>, is defined as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \int \limits _{C}\mathbf {F} (\mathbf {r} )\cdot \,d\mathbf {r} =\int _{a}^{b}\mathbf {F} (\mathbf {r} (t))\cdot \mathbf {r} '(t)\,dt.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \int \limits _{C}\mathbf {F} (\mathbf {r} )\cdot \,d\mathbf {r} =\int _{a}^{b}\mathbf {F} (\mathbf {r} (t))\cdot \mathbf {r} '(t)\,dt.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\int \limits _{C}\mathbf {F} (\mathbf {r} )\cdot \,d\mathbf {r} =\int _{a}^{b}\mathbf {F} (\mathbf {r} (t))\cdot \mathbf {r} '(t)\,dt." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3229bfe133c09a4024015237d836e94d73462201" style="vertical-align: -4.005ex; margin-left: -0.031ex; width:34.924ex; height:8.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where · is the <a href="/wiki/Dot_product" title="Dot product">dot product</a> and <b>r</b>: [a, b] → <i>C</i> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bijective" title="Bijective">bijective</a> <a href="/wiki/Parametric_equation" title="Parametric equation">parametrization</a> of the curve <i>C</i> such that <b>r</b>(<i>a</i>) and <b>r</b>(<i>b</i>) give the endpoints of <i>C</i>.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Surface_integral" title="Surface integral">surface integral</a>  is a generalization of <a href="/wiki/Multiple_integral" title="Multiple integral">multiple integrals</a> to integration over <a href="/wiki/Surface_(topology)" title="Surface (topology)">surfaces</a>. It can be thought of as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_integral" title="Double integral">double integral</a> analog of the <a href="/wiki/Line_integral" title="Line integral">line integral</a>. To find an explicit formula for the surface integral, we need to <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_system" title="Coordinate system">parameterize</a> the surface of interest, <i>S</i>, by considering a system of <a href="/wiki/Curvilinear_coordinates" title="Curvilinear coordinates">curvilinear coordinates</a> on <i>S</i>, like the <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">latitude and longitude</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a>. Let such a parameterization be <b>x</b>(<i>s</i>, <i>t</i>), where (<i>s</i>, <i>t</i>) varies in some region <i>T</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#Cartesian_coordinates_in_two_dimensions" title="Cartesian coordinate system">plane</a>. Then, the surface integral is given by
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \iint _{S}f\,\mathrm {d} S=\iint _{T}f(\mathbf {x} (s,t))\left\|{\partial \mathbf {x}  \over \partial s}\times {\partial \mathbf {x}  \over \partial t}\right\|\mathrm {d} s\,\mathrm {d} t}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \iint _{S}f\,\mathrm {d} S=\iint _{T}f(\mathbf {x} (s,t))\left\|{\partial \mathbf {x}  \over \partial s}\times {\partial \mathbf {x}  \over \partial t}\right\|\mathrm {d} s\,\mathrm {d} t}</annotation>
</semantics>

<p>where the expression between bars on the right-hand side is the <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)" title="Magnitude (mathematics)">magnitude</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Cross_product" title="Cross product">cross product</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Partial_derivative" title="Partial derivative">partial derivatives</a> of <b>x</b>(<i>s</i>, <i>t</i>), and is known as the surface <a href="/wiki/Volume_element" title="Volume element">element</a>. Given a vector field <b>v</b> on <i>S</i>, that is a function that assigns to each <b>x</b> in <i>S</i> a vector <b>v</b>(<b>x</b>), the surface integral can be defined component-wise according to the definition of the surface integral of a scalar field; the result is a vector.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Volume_integral" title="Volume integral">volume integral</a> refers to an <a href="/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">integral</a> over a 3-<a href="/wiki/Dimension" title="Dimension">dimensional</a> domain.
</p><p>It can also mean a <a href="/wiki/Multiple_integral" title="Multiple integral">triple integral</a> within a region <i>D</i> in <b>R</b><sup>3</sup> of a <a href="/wiki/Function_(mathematics)" title="Function (mathematics)">function</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle f(x,y,z),}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \iiint \limits _{D}f(x,y,z)\,dx\,dy\,dz.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fundamental_theorem_of_line_integrals">Fundamental theorem of line integrals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Fundamental theorem of line integrals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_line_integrals" title="Fundamental theorem of line integrals">Fundamental theorem of line integrals</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_line_integrals" title="Fundamental theorem of line integrals">fundamental theorem of line integrals</a>, says that a <a href="/wiki/Line_integral" title="Line integral">line integral</a> through a <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">gradient</a> field can be evaluated by evaluating the original scalar field at the endpoints of the curve.
</p><p>Let <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \varphi :U\subseteq \mathbb {R} ^{n}\to \mathbb {R} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>

Then

<h3><span id="Stokes.27_theorem"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Stokes'_theorem">Stokes' theorem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Stokes' theorem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stokes%27_theorem" title="Stokes' theorem">Stokes' theorem</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stokes%27_theorem" title="Stokes' theorem">Stokes' theorem</a> relates the <a href="/wiki/Surface_integral" title="Surface integral">surface integral</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Curl_(mathematics)" title="Curl (mathematics)">curl</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Vector_field" title="Vector field">vector field</a> F over a surface Σ in Euclidean three-space to the <a href="/wiki/Line_integral" title="Line integral">line integral</a> of the vector field over its boundary ∂Σ:
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Divergence_theorem">Divergence theorem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Divergence theorem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Divergence_theorem" title="Divergence theorem">Divergence theorem</a></div>
<p>Suppose <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">V</span> is a subset of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{n}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{n}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} ^{n}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c510b63578322050121fe966f2e5770bea43308d" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.897ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> (in the case of <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i> = 3, <i>V</i></span> represents a volume in 3D space) which is <a href="/wiki/Compact_space" title="Compact space">compact</a> and has a <a href="/wiki/Piecewise" title="Piecewise">piecewise</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smooth_surface" title="Smooth surface">smooth boundary</a> <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">S</span> (also indicated with <span class="texhtml">∂<i>V</i> = <i>S</i> </span>). If <span class="texhtml"><b>F</b></span> is a continuously differentiable vector field defined on a neighborhood of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">V</span>, then the <a href="/wiki/Divergence_theorem" title="Divergence theorem">divergence theorem</a> says:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spiegel_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-spiegel-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \scriptstyle S}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\scriptstyle S" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/62e91bf22a258bad807b5a9ae8068ef38202ca30" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.06ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span></span> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle (\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathbf {n} )\,dS.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">n</mi>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mi>d</mi>
<mi>S</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathbf {n} )\,dS.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="(\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathbf {n} )\,dS." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a092de3b4f8ef65be65f520b5a57bf22dd8474ee" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:10.405ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The left side is a <a href="/wiki/Volume_integral" title="Volume integral">volume integral</a> over the volume <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">V</span>, the right side is the <a href="/wiki/Surface_integral" title="Surface integral">surface integral</a> over the boundary of the volume <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">V</span>. The closed manifold <span class="texhtml">∂<i>V</i></span> is quite generally the boundary of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">V</span> oriented by outward-pointing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_normal" title="Surface normal">normals</a>, and <span class="texhtml"><b>n</b></span> is the outward pointing unit normal field of the boundary <span class="texhtml">∂<i>V</i></span>. (<span class="texhtml"><i>d</i><b>S</b></span> may be used as a shorthand for <span class="texhtml"><b>n</b><i>dS</i></span>.)
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_topology">In topology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: In topology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><span class="mw-3d-wrapper" data-label="3D"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WikipediaGlobeOnePiece.stl"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2880" data-file-width="5120" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/WikipediaGlobeOnePiece.stl/260px-WikipediaGlobeOnePiece.stl.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/WikipediaGlobeOnePiece.stl/390px-WikipediaGlobeOnePiece.stl.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/WikipediaGlobeOnePiece.stl/520px-WikipediaGlobeOnePiece.stl.png 2x" width="260"/></a></span> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WikipediaGlobeOnePiece.stl" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia" title="Wikipedia">Wikipedia</a>'s globe logo in 3-D</div></div></div>
<p>Three-dimensional space has a number of topological properties that distinguish it from spaces of other dimension numbers. For example, at least three dimensions are required to tie a <a href="/wiki/Knot_theory" title="Knot theory">knot</a> in a piece of string.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">differential geometry</a> the generic three-dimensional spaces are <a href="/wiki/3-manifold" title="3-manifold">3-manifolds</a>, which locally resemble <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\mathbb {R} }^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\mathbb {R} }^{3}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\mathbb {R} }^{3}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d5a6b0738d188535841b461617003c7ef7bdb816" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.732ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_finite_geometry">In finite geometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Three-dimensional_space&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: In finite geometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many ideas of dimension can be tested with <a href="/wiki/Finite_geometry" title="Finite geometry">finite geometry</a>. The simplest instance is <a href="/wiki/PG(3,2)" title="PG(3,2)">PG(3,2)</a>, which has <a href="/wiki/Fano_plane" title="Fano plane">Fano planes</a> as its 2-dimensional subspaces. It is an instance of <a href="/wiki/Galois_geometry" title="Galois geometry">Galois geometry</a>, a study of <a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a> using <a href="/wiki/Finite_field" title="Finite field">finite fields</a>. Thus, for any Galois field GF(<i>q</i>), there is a <a href="/wiki/Projective_space" title="Projective space">projective space</a> PG(3,<i>q</i>) of three dimensions. For example, any three <a href="/wiki/Skew_lines" title="Skew lines">skew lines</a> in PG(3,<i>q</i>) are contained in exactly one <a href="/wiki/Regulus_(geometry)" title="Regulus (geometry)">regulus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
